<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Flower</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><span class="flaggedrevs-icon oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-articleSearch oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-iconWidget" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."></span><span class="fr-toggle-arrow oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicator-down oo-ui-indicatorElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-indicatorWidget" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" title="show/hide details"></span></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Flower">reviewed</a> on <i>5 October 2022</i>.</div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Flower_(disambiguation)" title="Flower (disambiguation)">Flower (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Floral" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Floral_(disambiguation)" title="Floral (disambiguation)">Floral (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Reproductive structure in flowering plants</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flower_poster_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5932" data-file-width="4462" decoding="async" height="439" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Flower_poster_2.jpg/330px-Flower_poster_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Flower_poster_2.jpg/495px-Flower_poster_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Flower_poster_2.jpg/660px-Flower_poster_2.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flower_poster_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flowers or clusters of flowers produced by twelve species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">Angiosperms</a> from different families.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2376" data-file-width="1792" decoding="async" height="438" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg/330px-Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg/495px-Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg/660px-Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bl%C3%BCten_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Selection of differently constructed flowers at different stages of <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plant</a> development</div></div></div><p>A <b>flower</b>, sometimes known as a <b>bloom</b> or <b><a href="/wiki/Blossom" title="Blossom">blossom</a></b>, is the <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproductive</a> structure found in <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a> (plants of the division <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiospermae" title="Angiospermae">Angiospermae</a>). The biological function of a flower is to facilitate reproduction, usually by providing a mechanism for the union of <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a> with <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">eggs</a>. Flowers may facilitate outcrossing (fusion of sperm and eggs from different individuals in a population) resulting from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-pollination" title="Cross-pollination">cross-pollination</a> or allow selfing (fusion of sperm and egg from the same flower) when <a href="/wiki/Self-pollination" title="Self-pollination">self-pollination</a> occurs.
</p><p>There are two types of <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a>: self-pollination and cross-pollination. Self-pollination occurs when the <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anther" title="Anther">anther</a> is deposited on the <a href="/wiki/Stigma_(botany)" title="Stigma (botany)">stigma</a> of the same flower, or another flower on the same plant. Cross-pollination is when pollen is transferred from the anther of one flower to the stigma of another flower on a different individual of the same species. Self-pollination happens in flowers where the <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">stamen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpel" title="Carpel">carpel</a> mature at the same time, and are positioned so that the pollen can land on the flower's stigma. This pollination does not require an investment from the plant to provide nectar and pollen as food for pollinators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some flowers produce <a href="/wiki/Diaspore_(botany)" title="Diaspore (botany)">diaspores</a> without fertilization (<a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpy" title="Parthenocarpy">parthenocarpy</a>). Flowers contain <a href="/wiki/Sporangium" title="Sporangium">sporangia</a> and are the site where <a href="/wiki/Gametophyte" title="Gametophyte">gametophytes</a> develop. 
</p><p>Many flowers have evolved to be attractive to animals, so as to cause them to be vectors for the transfer of <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>. After fertilization, the <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovary</a> of the flower develops into <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">seeds</a>.
</p><p>In addition to facilitating the reproduction of flowering plants, flowers have long been admired and used by humans to bring beauty to the environment, and also as objects of romance, ritual, esotericism, <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft" title="Witchcraft">witchcraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, holistic medicine, and as a source of <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Flower is from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <span title="Middle English-language text"><i lang="enm">flour</i></span>, which referred to both the <a href="/wiki/Flour" title="Flour">ground grain</a> and the reproductive structure in plants, before splitting off in the 17th century. It comes originally from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name of the Italian goddess of flowers, <a href="/wiki/Flora_(mythology)" title="Flora (mythology)">Flora</a>. The early word for flower in English was <i>blossom</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> though it now refers to flowers only of <a href="/wiki/Fruit_tree" title="Fruit tree">fruit trees</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:391px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mature_flower_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="693" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Mature_flower_diagram.svg/389px-Mature_flower_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Mature_flower_diagram.svg/584px-Mature_flower_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Mature_flower_diagram.svg/778px-Mature_flower_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="389"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mature_flower_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of flower parts.</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Floral_morphology" title="Floral morphology">Floral morphology</a></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Floral_morphology"></span>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> of a flower, or its form and structure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> can be considered in two parts: the vegetative part, consisting of non-reproductive structures such as <a href="/wiki/Petal" title="Petal">petals</a>; and the reproductive or sexual parts. A stereotypical flower is made up of four kinds of structures attached to the tip of a short stalk or axis, called a <a href="/wiki/Receptacle_(botany)" title="Receptacle (botany)">receptacle</a>. Each of these parts or floral organs is arranged in a spiral called a <a href="/wiki/Whorl_(botany)" title="Whorl (botany)">whorl</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20104_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20104-5">[5]</a></sup> The four main whorls (starting from the base of the flower or lowest node and working upwards) are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calyx_(botany)" title="Calyx (botany)">calyx</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corolla_(botany)" title="Corolla (botany)">corolla</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Androecium" title="Androecium">androecium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gynoecium" title="Gynoecium">gynoecium</a>. Together the calyx and corolla make up the non-reproductive part of the flower called the <a href="/wiki/Perianth" title="Perianth">perianth</a>, and in some cases may not be differentiated. If this is the case, then they are described as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tepals" title="Tepals">tepals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20105–6_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20105–6-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Perianth">Perianth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Perianth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Perianth" title="Perianth">Perianth</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Calyx">Calyx</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Calyx">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sepal" title="Sepal">sepals</a>, collectively called the calyx, are modified <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a> that occur on the outermost whorl of the flower. They are leaf-like, in that they have a broad base, <a href="/wiki/Stoma" title="Stoma">stomata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stipule" title="Stipule">stipules</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20107_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20107-7">[7]</a></sup> Sepals are often waxy and tough, and grow quickly to protect the flower as it develops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20107_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20107-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016225_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016225-8">[8]</a></sup> They may be <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a>, but will more commonly grow on to assist in fruit dispersal. If the calyx is fused together it is called gamosepalous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20107_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20107-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Corolla">Corolla</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Corolla">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Petal" title="Petal">petals</a>, together the corolla, are almost or completely fiberless leaf-like structures that form the innermost whorl of the perianth. They are often delicate and thin, and are usually coloured, shaped, or scented to encourage pollination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016226_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016226-9">[9]</a></sup> Although similar to leaves in shape, they are more comparable to stamens in that they form almost simultaneously with one another, but their subsequent growth is delayed. If the corolla is fused together it is called sympetalous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20108_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20108-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive">Reproductive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Reproductive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plant_reproductive_morphology" title="Plant reproductive morphology">Plant reproductive morphology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lillium_Stamens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="1296" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Lillium_Stamens.jpg/220px-Lillium_Stamens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Lillium_Stamens.jpg/330px-Lillium_Stamens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Lillium_Stamens.jpg/440px-Lillium_Stamens.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lillium_Stamens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reproductive parts of Easter Lily (<i><a href="/wiki/Lilium_longiflorum" title="Lilium longiflorum">Lilium longiflorum</a></i>). 1. Stigma, 2. Style, 3. Stamens, 4. Filament, 5. Petal</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Androecium">Androecium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Androecium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Androecium" title="Androecium">androecium</a>, or stamens, is the whorl of pollen producing male parts. Stamens consist typically of an anther, made up of four pollen sacs arranged in two <a href="/wiki/Theca" title="Theca">thecae</a>, connected to a filament, or stalk. The anther contains microsporocytes which become <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, the male <a href="/wiki/Gametophyte" title="Gametophyte">gametophyte</a>, after undergoing <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>. Although they exhibit the widest variation among floral organs, the androecium is usually confined just to one whorl and to two whorls only in rare cases. Stamens range in number, size, shape, orientation, and in their point of connection to the flower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016226_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016226-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20108_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.20108-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gynoecium">Gynoecium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Gynoecium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gynoecium" title="Gynoecium">gynoecium</a>, or the carpels, is the female part of the flower found on the innermost whorl. Each carpel consists of a <a href="/wiki/Stigma_(botany)" title="Stigma (botany)">stigma</a>, which receives pollen, a style, which acts as a stalk, and an <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">ovary</a>, which contains the ovules. Carpels may occur in one to several whorls, and when fused together are often described as a pistil. Inside the ovary, the <a href="/wiki/Ovule" title="Ovule">ovules</a> are suspended off of pieces of tissue called <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016229_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016229-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201014_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201014-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variation">Variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although this arrangement is considered "typical", plant species show a wide variation in floral structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sattler73_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sattler73-13">[13]</a></sup> The four main parts of a flower are generally defined by their positions on the receptacle and not by their function. Many flowers lack some parts or parts may be modified into other functions or look like what is typically another part.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In some families, like <a href="/wiki/Ranunculaceae" title="Ranunculaceae">Ranunculaceae</a>, the petals are greatly reduced and in many species the sepals are colorful and petal-like. Other flowers have modified stamens that are petal-like; the double flowers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peonie" title="Peonie">Peonies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">Roses</a> are mostly petaloid stamens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many flowers have a <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">symmetry</a>. When the <a href="/wiki/Perianth" title="Perianth">perianth</a> is bisected through the central axis from any point and symmetrical halves are produced, the flower is said to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinomorphic" title="Actinomorphic">actinomorphic</a> or regular. This is an example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial_symmetry" title="Radial symmetry">radial symmetry</a>. When flowers are bisected and produce only one line that produces symmetrical halves, the flower is said to be irregular or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zygomorphic" title="Zygomorphic">zygomorphic</a>. If, in rare cases, they have no symmetry at all they are called asymmetric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201025_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201025-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flowers may be directly attached to the plant at their base (<a href="/wiki/Sessility_(botany)" title="Sessility (botany)">sessile</a>—the supporting stalk or stem is highly reduced or absent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016243_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016243-18">[18]</a></sup> The stem or stalk subtending a flower, or an <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> of flowers, is called a <a href="/wiki/Peduncle_(botany)" title="Peduncle (botany)">peduncle</a>. If a peduncle supports more than one flower, the stems connecting each flower to the main axis are called <a href="/wiki/Pedicel_(botany)" title="Pedicel (botany)">pedicels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.2010410_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.2010410-19">[19]</a></sup> The apex of a flowering stem forms a terminal swelling which is called the torus or receptacle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the majority of species individual flowers have both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pistil" title="Pistil">pistils</a> and stamens. These flowers are described by botanists as being perfect, bisexual, or <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a>. However, in some species of plants the flowers are imperfect or unisexual: having only either male (stamens) or female (pistil) parts. In the latter case, if an individual plant is either female or male the species is regarded as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_sexuality" title="Plant sexuality">dioecious</a>. However, where unisexual male and female flowers appear on the same plant, the species is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_sexuality" title="Plant sexuality">monoecious</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016239_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016239-20">[20]</a></sup> Many flowers have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nectaries" title="Nectaries">nectaries</a>, which are glands that produce a sugary fluid used to attract pollinators. They are not considered as an organ on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201021_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201021-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflorescence">Inflorescence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Inflorescence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">Inflorescence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:White_and_yellow_flower.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/White_and_yellow_flower.JPG/220px-White_and_yellow_flower.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/White_and_yellow_flower.JPG/330px-White_and_yellow_flower.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/White_and_yellow_flower.JPG/440px-White_and_yellow_flower.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:White_and_yellow_flower.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Zantedeschia_aethiopica" title="Zantedeschia aethiopica">calla lily</a> is not a single flower. It is actually an <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a> of tiny flowers pressed together on a central stalk that is surrounded by a large petal-like <a href="/wiki/Bract" title="Bract">bract</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div><p>In those species that have more than one flower on an axis, the collective cluster of flowers is called an <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a>. Some inflorescences are composed of many small flowers arranged in a formation that resembles a single flower. The common example of this is most members of the very large composite (<a href="/wiki/Asteraceae" title="Asteraceae">Asteraceae</a>) group. A single <a href="/wiki/Asteraceae" title="Asteraceae">daisy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunflower" title="Sunflower">sunflower</a>, for example, is not a flower but a flower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_(botany)" title="Head (botany)">head</a>—an inflorescence composed of numerous flowers (or florets).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016228_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016228-23">[23]</a></sup> An inflorescence may include specialized stems and modified leaves known as <a href="/wiki/Bract" title="Bract">bracts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201022–24_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201022–24-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Floral_diagrams_and_formulae">Floral diagrams and formulae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Floral diagrams and formulae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Floral_formula" title="Floral formula">Floral formula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Floral_diagram" title="Floral diagram">Floral diagram</a></div>
<p>A <i>floral formula</i> is a way to represent the structure of a flower using specific letters, numbers and symbols, presenting substantial information about the flower in a compact form. It can represent a <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a>, usually giving ranges of the numbers of different organs, or particular species. Floral formulae have been developed in the early 19th century and their use has declined since. Prenner <i>et al.</i> (2010) devised an extension of the existing model to broaden the descriptive capability of the formula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prenner_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-prenner-25">[25]</a></sup> The format of floral formulae differs in different parts of the world, yet they convey the same information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201038_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201038-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharma2009165–166_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma2009165–166-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>The structure of a flower can also be expressed by the means of <a href="/wiki/Floral_diagram" title="Floral diagram">floral diagrams</a>. The use of schematic diagrams can replace long descriptions or complicated drawings as a tool for understanding both floral structure and evolution. Such diagrams may show important features of flowers, including the relative positions of the various organs, including the presence of fusion and symmetry, as well as structural details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201036_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_CraeneP.201036-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A flower develops on a modified shoot or <i>axis</i> from a determinate apical <a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristem</a> (<i>determinate</i> meaning the axis grows to a set size). It has compressed internodes, bearing structures that in classical plant morphology are interpreted as highly modified <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Detailed developmental studies, however, have shown that stamens are often initiated more or less like <a href="/wiki/Aerial_stem_modification" title="Aerial stem modification">modified stems</a> (caulomes) that in some cases may even resemble <a href="/wiki/Branchlet" title="Branchlet">branchlets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sattler73_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sattler73-13">[13]</a></sup> Taking into account the whole diversity in the development of the androecium of flowering plants, we find a continuum between modified leaves (phyllomes), modified stems (caulomes), and modified branchlets (shoots).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition">Transition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Transition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
The transition to flowering is one of the major phase changes that a plant makes during its life cycle. The transition must take place at a time that is favorable for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">fertilization</a> and the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seeds" title="Seeds">seeds</a>, hence ensuring maximal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive" title="Reproductive">reproductive</a> success. To meet these needs a plant is able to interpret important endogenous and environmental cues such as changes in levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_hormones" title="Plant hormones">plant hormones</a> and seasonable <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoperiod" title="Photoperiod">photoperiod</a> changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ausin2005_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ausin2005-35">[35]</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial</a> and most <a href="/wiki/Biennial_plant" title="Biennial plant">biennial</a> plants require <a href="/wiki/Vernalization" title="Vernalization">vernalization</a> to flower. The molecular interpretation of these signals is through the transmission of a complex signal known as <a href="/wiki/Florigen" title="Florigen">florigen</a>, which involves a variety of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, including Constans, Flowering Locus C and Flowering Locus T. Florigen is produced in the leaves in reproductively favorable conditions and acts in <a href="/wiki/Bud" title="Bud">buds</a> and growing tips to induce a number of different physiological and morphological changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turck2008_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turck2008-36">[36]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ABC_flower_development.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="363" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ABC_flower_development.svg/170px-ABC_flower_development.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ABC_flower_development.svg/255px-ABC_flower_development.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ABC_flower_development.svg/340px-ABC_flower_development.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ABC_flower_development.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ABC model of flower development</div></div></div><p>The first step of the transition is the transformation of the vegetative stem primordia into floral primordia. This occurs as biochemical changes take place to change cellular differentiation of leaf, bud and stem tissues into tissue that will grow into the reproductive organs. Growth of the central part of the stem tip stops or flattens out and the sides develop protuberances in a whorled or spiral fashion around the outside of the stem end. These protuberances develop into the sepals, petals, stamens, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carpel" title="Carpel">carpels</a>. Once this process begins, in most plants, it cannot be reversed and the stems develop flowers, even if the initial start of the flower formation event was dependent of some environmental cue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle2006_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Searle2006-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organ_development">Organ development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Organ development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/ABC_model_of_flower_development" title="ABC model of flower development">ABC model of flower development</a></div>
<p>The ABC model is a simple model that describes the <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> responsible for the development of flowers. Three gene activities interact in a combinatorial manner to determine the developmental identities of the <a href="/wiki/Primordium" title="Primordium">primordia</a> organ within the floral <a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">apical meristem</a>. These gene functions are called A, B, and C. A genes are expressed in only outer and lower most section of the apical meristem, which becomes a whorl of sepals. In the second whorl both A and B genes are expressed, leading to the formation of petals. In the third whorl, B and C genes interact to form stamens and in the center of the flower C genes alone give rise to carpels. The model is based upon studies of aberrant flowers and mutations in <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis_thaliana" title="Arabidopsis thaliana">Arabidopsis thaliana</a></i> and the snapdragon, <i><a href="/wiki/Antirrhinum_majus" title="Antirrhinum majus">Antirrhinum majus</a></i>. For example, when there is a loss of B gene function, mutant flowers are produced with sepals in the first whorl as usual, but also in the second whorl instead of the normal petal formation. In the third whorl the lack of B function but presence of C function mimics the fourth whorl, leading to the formation of carpels also in the third whorl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016392–395_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016392–395-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Function">Function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plant_reproductive_morphology" title="Plant reproductive morphology">Plant reproductive morphology</a></div>
<p>The principal purpose of a flower is the reproduction of the individual and the species. All flowering plants are <i><a href="/wiki/Heterospory" title="Heterospory">heterosporous</a></i>, that is, every individual plant produces two types of <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a>. Microspores are produced by <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> inside anthers and megaspores are produced inside ovules that are within an ovary. Anthers typically consist of four microsporangia and an ovule is an integumented megasporangium. Both types of spores develop into <a href="/wiki/Gametophyte" title="Gametophyte">gametophytes</a> inside sporangia. As with all heterosporous plants, the gametophytes also develop inside the spores, i. e., they are endosporic.
</p><p>In the majority of plant species, individual flowers have both functional carpels and stamens. Botanists describe these flowers as perfect or bisexual, and the species as <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphroditic</a>. In a minority of plant species, their flowers lack one or the other reproductive organ and are described as imperfect or unisexual. If the individual plants of a species each have unisexual flowers of both sexes then the species is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoecious" title="Monoecious">monoecious</a>. Alternatively, if each individual plant has only unisexual flowers of the same sex then the species is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioecious" title="Dioecious">dioecious</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollination">Pollination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pollination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">Pollination</a></div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tui_03.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1508" data-file-width="2010" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tui_03.jpg/200px-Tui_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tui_03.jpg/300px-Tui_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Tui_03.jpg/400px-Tui_03.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A Tūī, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosthemadera_novaeseelandiae" title="Prosthemadera novaeseelandiae">Prosthemadera novaeseelandiae</a></i>, feeding on flax flower nectar, with yellow pollen on its forehead</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bees_Collecting_Pollen_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="386" data-file-width="439" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Bees_Collecting_Pollen_cropped.jpg/200px-Bees_Collecting_Pollen_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Bees_Collecting_Pollen_cropped.jpg/300px-Bees_Collecting_Pollen_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Bees_Collecting_Pollen_cropped.jpg/400px-Bees_Collecting_Pollen_cropped.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Grains of pollen sticking to this bee will be transferred to the next flower it visits.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The primary purpose of the flower is <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Since the flowers are the reproductive organs of the plant, they mediate the joining of the sperm, contained within pollen, to the ovules — contained in the ovary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016225_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016225-8">[8]</a></sup> Pollination is the movement of pollen from the anthers to the stigma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker20209_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker20209-40">[40]</a></sup> Normally pollen is moved from one plant to another, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-pollination" title="Cross-pollination">cross-pollination</a>, but many plants are able to self-pollinate. Cross-pollination is preferred because it allows for <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variation" title="Genetic variation">genetic variation</a>, which contributes to the survival of the species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238-41">[41]</a></sup> Many flowers are dependent, then, upon external factors for pollination, such as: the wind, water, animals, and especially <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>. Larger animals such as birds, bats, and even some <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_possum" title="Pygmy possum">pygmy possums</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> however, can also be employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202065_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202065-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202069–83_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202069–83-44">[44]</a></sup> To accomplish this, flowers have specific designs which encourage the transfer of pollen from one plant to another of the same species. The period of time during which this process can take place (when the flower is fully expanded and functional) is called <i><a href="/wiki/Anthesis" title="Anthesis">anthesis</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2020120_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2020120-45">[45]</a></sup> hence the study of pollination biology is called <i><a href="/wiki/Anthecology" title="Anthecology">anthecology</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flowering plants usually face <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure" title="Evolutionary pressure">evolutionary pressure</a> to optimize the transfer of their <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, and this is typically reflected in the morphology of the flowers and the behaviour of the plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Pollen may be transferred between plants via a number of 'vectors,' or methods. Around 80% of flowering plants make use of biotic, or living vectors. Others use abiotic, or non-living, vectors and some plants make use of multiple vectors, but most are highly specialised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though some fit between or outside of these groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202046_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202046-49">[49]</a></sup> most flowers can be divided between the following two broad groups of pollination methods:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biotic_pollination">Biotic pollination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Biotic pollination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Flowers that use biotic vectors attract and use <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, or other animals to transfer pollen from one flower to the next. Often they are <a href="/wiki/Generalist_and_specialist_species" title="Generalist and specialist species">specialized</a> in shape and have an arrangement of the stamens that ensures that pollen grains are transferred to the bodies of the pollinator when it lands in search of its attractant (such as nectar, pollen, or a mate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016239–240_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016239–240-50">[50]</a></sup> In pursuing this attractant from many flowers of the same species, the pollinator transfers pollen to the stigmas—arranged with equally pointed precision—of all of the flowers it visits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202068_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202068-51">[51]</a></sup> Many flowers rely on simple proximity between flower parts to ensure pollination, while others have elaborate designs to ensure pollination and prevent <a href="/wiki/Self-pollination" title="Self-pollination">self-pollination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238-41">[41]</a></sup> Flowers use animals including: insects (<i><a href="/wiki/Entomophily" title="Entomophily">entomophily</a></i>), birds (<i><a href="/wiki/Ornithophily" title="Ornithophily">ornithophily</a></i>), bats (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiropterophily" title="Chiropterophily">chiropterophily</a></i>), lizards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202069–83_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202069–83-44">[44]</a></sup> and even snails and slugs (<i>malacophilae</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190672–80_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190672–80-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attraction_methods">Attraction methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Attraction methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:220px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="745" data-file-width="595" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg/218px-Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg/327px-Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg/436px-Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg 2x" width="218"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ophrys_apifera_flower1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Ophrys_apifera" title="Ophrys apifera">Ophrys apifera</a></i>, a bee orchid, which has evolved over many generations to mimic a female bee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Plants cannot move from one location to another, thus many flowers have evolved to attract animals to transfer pollen between individuals in dispersed populations. Most commonly, flowers are insect-pollinated, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Entomophily" title="Entomophily">entomophilous</a></i>; literally "insect-loving" in Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202081_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202081-54">[54]</a></sup> To attract these insects flowers commonly have glands called <i>nectaries</i> on various parts that attract animals looking for nutritious <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2020112–113_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2020112–113-55">[55]</a></sup> Birds and bees have <a href="/wiki/Color_vision" title="Color vision">color vision</a>, enabling them to seek out "colorful" flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2020107–108_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2020107–108-56">[56]</a></sup> Some flowers have patterns, called <a href="/wiki/Nectar_guide" title="Nectar guide">nectar guides</a>, that show pollinators where to look for nectar; they may be visible only under <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light, which is visible to bees and some other insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2020121_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2020121-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flowers also attract pollinators by <a href="/wiki/Floral_scent" title="Floral scent">scent</a>, though not all flower scents are appealing to humans; a number of flowers are pollinated by insects that are attracted to rotten flesh and have flowers that smell like dead animals. These are often called <a href="/wiki/Carrion_flower" title="Carrion flower">Carrion flowers</a>, including plants in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Rafflesia" title="Rafflesia">Rafflesia</a></i>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_arum" title="Titan arum">titan arum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2020107–108_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2020107–108-56">[56]</a></sup> Flowers pollinated by night visitors, including bats and moths, are likely to concentrate on scent to attract pollinators and so most such flowers are white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker2020173_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2020173-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flowers are also specialized in shape and have an arrangement of the <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">stamens</a> that ensures that pollen grains are transferred to the bodies of the pollinator when it lands in search of its attractant. Other flowers use mimicry or <a href="/wiki/Pseudocopulation" title="Pseudocopulation">pseudocopulation</a> to attract pollinators. Many orchids for example, produce flowers resembling female bees or wasps in colour, shape, and scent. Males move from one flower to the next in search of a mate, pollinating the flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016241_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016241-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollinator_relationships">Pollinator relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pollinator relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Pollination_syndrome" title="Pollination syndrome">Pollination syndrome</a></div>
<p>Many flowers have close relationships with one or a few specific pollinating organisms. Many flowers, for example, attract only one specific species of insect, and therefore rely on that insect for successful reproduction. This close relationship an example of <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">coevolution</a>, as the flower and pollinator have developed together over a long period of time to match each other's needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016240_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016240-61">[61]</a></sup> This close relationship compounds the negative effects of <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>, however, since the extinction of either member in such a relationship would almost certainly mean the extinction of the other member as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abiotic_pollination">Abiotic pollination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Abiotic pollination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Anemophily" title="Anemophily">Anemophily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrophily" title="Hydrophily">Hydrophily</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grassflower.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="353" data-file-width="435" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Grassflower.svg/200px-Grassflower.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Grassflower.svg/300px-Grassflower.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Grassflower.svg/400px-Grassflower.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A Grass flower with its long, thin filaments and large feathery stigma.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enhalus_acoroides_fleur_femelle.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="368" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Enhalus_acoroides_fleur_femelle.jpg/200px-Enhalus_acoroides_fleur_femelle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Enhalus_acoroides_fleur_femelle.jpg/300px-Enhalus_acoroides_fleur_femelle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Enhalus_acoroides_fleur_femelle.jpg/400px-Enhalus_acoroides_fleur_femelle.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The female flower of <i>Enhalus acoroides</i>, which is pollinated through a combination of Hyphydrogamy and Ephydrogamy.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Flowers that use abiotic, or non-living, vectors use the <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> or, much less commonly, <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, to move pollen from one flower to the next.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">[48]</a></sup> In wind-dispersed (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anemophilous" title="Anemophilous">anemophilous</a>) species, the tiny pollen grains are carried, sometimes many thousands of kilometres,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> by the wind to other flowers. Common examples include the <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grasses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birch_trees" title="Birch trees">birch trees</a>, along with many other species in the order <i><a href="/wiki/Fagales" title="Fagales">fagales</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ragweed" title="Ragweed">ragweeds</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/Cyperaceae" title="Cyperaceae">sedges</a>. They have no need to attract pollinators and therefore tend not to grow large, showy, or colorful flowers, and do not have nectaries, nor a noticeable scent. Because of this, plants typically have many thousands of tiny flowers which have comparatively large, feathery stigmas; to increase the chance of pollen being received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016241_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016241-59">[59]</a></sup> Whereas the pollen of entomophilous flowers is usually large, sticky, and rich in <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> (to act as a "reward" for pollinators), anemophilous flower pollen is typically small-grained, very light, smooth, and of little nutritional value to <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190668–72_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190668–72-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In order for the wind to effectively pick up and transport the pollen, the flowers typically have anthers loosely attached to the end of long thin filaments, or pollen forms around a <a href="/wiki/Catkin" title="Catkin">catkin</a> which moves in the wind. Rarer forms of this involve individual flowers being moveable by the wind (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pendulous" title="Pendulous">Pendulous</a>), or even less commonly; the anthers exploding to release the pollen into the wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190668–72_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190668–72-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pollination through water (<a href="/wiki/Hydrophily" title="Hydrophily">hydrophily</a>) is a much rarer method, occurring in only around 2% of abiotically pollinated flowers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">[48]</a></sup> Common examples of this include <i>Calitriche autumnalis</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vallisneria_spiralis" title="Vallisneria spiralis">Vallisneria spiralis</a></i> and some <a href="/wiki/Seagrass" title="Seagrass">sea-grasses</a>. One characteristic which most species in this group share is a lack of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exine" title="Exine">exine</a>, or protective layer, around the pollen grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202051_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202051-67">[67]</a></sup> Paul Knuth identified two types of hydrophilous pollination in 1906 and Ernst Schwarzenbach added a third in 1944. Knuth named his two groups <i>Hyphydrogamy</i> and the more common <i>Ephydrogamy.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190668–69_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnuthMüllerAinsworth_Davis190668–69-68">[68]</a></sup> In <i>Hyphydrogamy</i> pollination occurs below the surface of the water and so the pollen grains are typically negatively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bouyant" title="Bouyant">buoyant</a>. For marine plants that exhibit this method the stigmas are usually stiff, while freshwater species have small and feathery stigmas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-69">[69]</a></sup> In <i>Ephydrogamy</i> pollination occurs on the surface of the water and so the pollen has a low density to enable floating, though many also use rafts, and are <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a>. Marine flowers have floating thread-like stigmas and may have adaptations for the tide, while freshwater species create indentations in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-69">[69]</a></sup> The third category, set out by Schwarzenbach, is those flowers which transport pollen above the water through conveyance. This ranges from floating plants, (<i><a href="/wiki/Lemnoideae" title="Lemnoideae">Lemnoideae</a></i>), to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staminate" title="Staminate">staminate</a> flowers (<i><a href="/wiki/Vallisneria" title="Vallisneria">Vallisneria</a></i>). Most species in this group have dry, spherical pollen which sometimes forms into larger masses, and female flowers which form depressions in the water; the method of transport varies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Flowers can be pollinated by two mechanisms; cross-pollination and self-pollination. No mechanism is indisputably better than the other as they each have their advantages and disadvantages. Plants use one or both of these mechanisms depending on their <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202036–37_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202036–37-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross-pollination">Cross-pollination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cross-pollination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cross-pollination is the pollination of the carpel by pollen from a different plant of the same species. Because the genetic make-up of the sperm contained within the pollen from the other plant is different, their combination will result in a new, genetically distinct, plant, through the process of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a>. Since each new plant is genetically distinct, the different plants show <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variation" title="Genetic variation">variation</a> in their physiological and structural adaptations and so the <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a> as a whole is better prepared for an adverse occurrence in the environment. Cross-pollination, therefore, increases the survival of the species and is usually preferred by flowers for this reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016222_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016222-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-pollination">Self-pollination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Self-pollination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clianthus_puniceus_(Kaka_Beak)_flowers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Clianthus_puniceus_%28Kaka_Beak%29_flowers.jpg/220px-Clianthus_puniceus_%28Kaka_Beak%29_flowers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Clianthus_puniceus_%28Kaka_Beak%29_flowers.jpg/330px-Clianthus_puniceus_%28Kaka_Beak%29_flowers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Clianthus_puniceus_%28Kaka_Beak%29_flowers.jpg/440px-Clianthus_puniceus_%28Kaka_Beak%29_flowers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clianthus_puniceus_(Kaka_Beak)_flowers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Clianthus puniceus</i>, the Kaka Beak.</div></div></div>
<p>Self-pollination is the pollination of the carpel of a flower by pollen from either the same flower or another flower on the same plant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016238-41">[41]</a></sup> leading to the creation of a genetic clone through <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual reproduction</a>. This increases the reliability of producing seeds, the rate at which they can be produced, and lowers the amount energy needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202042_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202042-72">[72]</a></sup> But, most importantly, it limits <a href="/wiki/Genetic_variation" title="Genetic variation">genetic variation</a>. 
</p><p>The extreme case of self-fertilization, when the ovule is fertilized by pollen from the same flower or plant, occurs in flowers that always self-fertilize, such as many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dandelion" title="Dandelion">dandelions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Some flowers are self-pollinated and have flowers that never open or are self-pollinated before the flowers open; these flowers are called <a href="/wiki/Cleistogamy" title="Cleistogamy">cleistogamous</a>; many species in the genus <a href="/wiki/Viola_(plant)" title="Viola (plant)"><i>Viola</i></a> exhibit this, for example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conversely, many species of plants have ways of preventing self-pollination and hence, self-fertilization. Unisexual male and female flowers on the same plant may not appear or mature at the same time, or pollen from the same plant may be incapable of fertilizing its ovules. The latter flower types, which have chemical barriers to their own pollen, are referred to as self-incompatible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016239_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016239-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202034_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202034-75">[75]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Clianthus_puniceus" title="Clianthus puniceus">Clianthus puniceus</a></i>, (pictured), self-pollination is used strategically as an "insurance policy." When a pollinator, in this case a bird, visits <i>C. puniceus</i> it rubs off the stigmatic covering and allows for pollen from the bird to enter the stigma. If no pollinators visit, however, then the stigmatic covering falls off naturally to allow for the flower's own anthers to pollinate the flower through self-pollination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalker202042_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker202042-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergies">Allergies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Allergies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pollen_allergy" title="Pollen allergy">Pollen allergy</a></div>
<p>Pollen is a large contributor to <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_allergy" title="Respiratory allergy">respiratory allergies</a> which combined affect between 10 and 50% of people worldwide. This number appears to be growing, as the temperature increases due to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> mean that plants are producing more pollen<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, which is also more allergenic. Pollen is difficult to avoid, however, because of its small size and prevalence in the natural environment. Most of the pollen which causes allergies is that produced by wind-dispersed pollinators such as the <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grasses</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birch_trees" title="Birch trees">birch trees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> trees, and <a href="/wiki/Ragweed" title="Ragweed">ragweeds</a>; the allergens in pollen are proteins which are thought to be necessary in the process of pollination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilization">Fertilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fertilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilization" title="Fertilization">Fertilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Double_fertilization" title="Double fertilization">Double fertilization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sex_(electronic_resource)_(1914)_(14590686978).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2118" data-file-width="1960" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Sex_%28electronic_resource%29_%281914%29_%2814590686978%29.jpg/200px-Sex_%28electronic_resource%29_%281914%29_%2814590686978%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Sex_%28electronic_resource%29_%281914%29_%2814590686978%29.jpg/300px-Sex_%28electronic_resource%29_%281914%29_%2814590686978%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Sex_%28electronic_resource%29_%281914%29_%2814590686978%29.jpg/400px-Sex_%28electronic_resource%29_%281914%29_%2814590686978%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sex_(electronic_resource)_(1914)_(14590686978).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A floral diagram, with the pollen tube labelled <i>PG</i></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fertilisation" title="Fertilisation">Fertilization</a>, also called <i>Synagmy,</i> occurs following pollination, which is the movement of pollen from the stamen to the carpel. It encompasses both <a href="/wiki/Plasmogamy" title="Plasmogamy">plasmogamy</a>, the fusion of the <a href="/wiki/Protoplast" title="Protoplast">protoplasts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karyogamy" title="Karyogamy">karyogamy</a>, the fusion of the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nuclei</a>. When pollen lands on the stigma of the flower it begins creating a <a href="/wiki/Pollen_tube" title="Pollen tube">pollen tube</a> which runs down through the style and into the ovary. After penetrating the centre-most part of the ovary it enters the egg apparatus and into one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synergid" title="Synergid">synergid</a>. At this point the end of the pollen tube bursts and releases the two sperm cells, one of which makes its way to an egg, while also losing its <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a> and much of its <a href="/wiki/Protoplasm" title="Protoplasm">protoplasm</a>. The sperm's nucleus then fuses with the egg's nucleus, resulting in the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> (two copies of each <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosome</a>) cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016234_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016234-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas in fertilization only plasmogamy, or the fusion of the whole sex cells, results, in Angiosperms (flowering plants) a process known as double fertilization, which involves both karyogamy and plasmogamy, occurs. In double fertilization the second sperm cell subsequently also enters the synergid and fuses with the two polar nuclei of the central cell. Since all three nuclei are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a>, they result in a large <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a> nucleus which is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triploid" title="Triploid">triploid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016234_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016234-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Seed_development">Seed development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Seed development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seed_development" title="Seed development">Seed development</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1896" data-file-width="2784" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg/220px-Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg/330px-Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg/440px-Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Autumn_Red_peaches.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fruit of a peach with the seed or stone inside.</div></div></div>
<p>Following the formation of zygote it begins to grow through nuclear and cellular divisions, called <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a>, eventually becoming a small group of cells. One section of it becomes the <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryo</a>, while the other becomes the suspensor; a structure which forces the embryo into the <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a> and is later undetectable. Two small <a href="/wiki/Primordium" title="Primordium">primordia</a> also form at this time, that later become the <a href="/wiki/Cotyledon" title="Cotyledon">cotyledon</a>, which is used as an energy store. Plants which grow out one of these primordia are called <a href="/wiki/Monocotyledon" title="Monocotyledon">monocotyledons</a>, while those that grow out two are <a href="/wiki/Dicotyledon" title="Dicotyledon">dicotyledons</a>. The next stage is called the Torpedo stage and involves the growth of several key structures, including: the <a href="/wiki/Radicle" title="Radicle">radicle</a> (embryotic root), the <a href="/wiki/Epicotyl" title="Epicotyl">epicotyl</a> (embryotic stem), and the <a href="/wiki/Hypocotyl" title="Hypocotyl">hypocotyl</a>, (the root/shoot junction). In the final step <a href="/wiki/Vascular_tissue" title="Vascular tissue">vascular tissue</a> develops around the seed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016235–237_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016235–237-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fruit_development">Fruit development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Fruit development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fruit#Development" title="Fruit">Fruit § Development</a></div>
<p>The ovary, inside which the seed is forming from the ovule, grows into a <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>. All the other main floral parts die during this development, including: the style, stigma, sepals, stamens, and petals. The fruit contains three structures: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exocarp" title="Exocarp">exocarp</a>, or outer layer, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesocarp" title="Mesocarp">mesocarp</a>, or the fleshy part, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endocarp" title="Endocarp">endocarp</a>, or innermost layer, while the fruit wall is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericarp" title="Pericarp">pericarp</a>. The size, shape, toughness, and thickness varies among different fruit. This is because it is directly connected to the method of seed dispersal; that being the purpose of fruit - to encourage or enable the seed's dispersal and protect the seed while doing so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016235–237_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED._Mauseth2016235–237-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Seed_dispersal">Seed dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Flower&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Seed dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biological_dispersal" title="Biological dispersal">Biological dispersal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seed_dispersal" title="Seed dispersal">Seed dispersal</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:612px;max-width:612px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image"
